 I. GALLIA est omnis divisa in partes tres, quarum unam
 incolunt Belge, aliam Aquitani, tertiam, qui ipsorum lingui
 Celtae, nostra Galli appellantur. Hi omnes lingua, institutis,
 legibus inter se diﬂ'erunt. Gallos ab Aquitanis Garumna
 (lumen, a Belgis Matrona et Sequana dividit. Horum om- 5
 nium fortissimi sunt Belgae, propterea quod a cultu atque

 trans Rhenum incolunt, quibuscum continenter bellum ge· 10
 runtz qua de causa Helvetii quoque reliquos Gallos virtute

 eorum finibus bellum gerunt. Eorum una pars, quam Gallos
 obtinere dictum et, initium capit a ﬂumine Rhodano ; con- 15
 tinetur Garumna flumine, Oceano, finibus Belgarum; attin-

 ad septentriones. Belgae ab extremis Galliae tinibus oriuntur;
 pertinent ad inferiorem partem fluminis Rheni ; spectant in
 septentriones, et orientem solem. Aquitania a Garumna 20
 ﬂumine ad Pyrenaeos montes et eam partem Oceani, quae est
 ad Hispaniam, pertinet; spectat inter occasum Solis et sep-
 tentriones.

 30. Vineae agere .· the vim were composed of wicker hurdles
 laid for a roof on the tops of posts, which the soldiers, who went
 under it for shelter, bore up with their hands. Some will have
 them to have been contrived with a double roof : the ﬁrst and
 lower roof of planks, and the Eimer roof of hurdles, to break the
 force of any blow without diso ering the machine—Under these
 the hesiegers either used the arias, or the battering ram, or tried ”
 to undermine the walls.

 33. Aggere .· the ag was a mound, composed of earth,
 wood, hurdles, and stone, w 'ch was gradually advanced towards
 the town, always increasing in he' ht, till it equalled or overtop d
 the walls. The agger was secure by towers, consisting of di er-
 ent stories, from which stones and darts were dischar d on the
 townsmen by means of engines—Mus: besides e towers
 belonging to the agger, there were two sorts of movable towers,
 the· lesser and the greater: the lesser sort were about sixty cu-
 bits high and the square sides seventeen cubits broad; they had
 ﬁve or six and sometimes ten stories of divisions, eve division
 being made open on all sides. The greater tower was lly20 cubils
 high, 23 cubita square, containing sometimes 15, sometimes 20
 divisions. They were of ve great use in making approaches to
 the wall, the divisionis being a le to carry soldiers, engines, ladders,
 casting-bridges and other necessaries. The wheels, on which
 they went, were contrived to be within the planks, to defend them
 from the enemy, and the men, who were to drive them forward,
 stood behind, where they were most secure: the soldiers in the

 ’ inside were protected by raw hides, which were thrown over the
 tower in those places which were most exposed.

 10. Sub sarcinis.- the load, which a Roman soldier carried, is
 almost incredible; provisions for 15 days, sometimes for more;
 utensils, viz. -a saw, a basket, a. inattock, an axe, a hook, at leathern
 thong, a pot, &c.; three or four. stakes, and frequently more;
 sometimes as many as twelve were added : the whole amounted
 to about sixty pounds weight, besides'arms; for a Roman soldier
 considered these not as a burden, but as a part of himself. Under
 this load, they commonly marched twenty miles a day, often a
 greater distance. There were beasts οἳ burden, and wagons for
 carrying tents, baggage, διο. (ἰτηρεάίπιοηία). On a march the
 bggtiage was generally placed either in the middle or in the rear
 o e army.

 17. Jiriu : the chief engine in the attack of places was the

 mice, or battering ram; this was of two sorts, the one rude and

 , ain, the other artiﬁcial and compound. The former seems to
 ave been nothing more than a great beam, which the soldiers
 bore on their arms and shoulders, and with one end of it, by main
 force, assailed the wall. The compound one was along· beam like
 the mast of a ship, armed at one end with iron in the form of a
 ram’s head. It was suspended by the middle with ropes and chains,
 fastened to the beam, that lay across two posts; hanging thus
 equally ballnced, it was thrust forward by a number of men, and
 drawn back : this was continued until the wall was battered down.

 ‚ 8. Certis ex aqua menum-ie .· ⁴ by accurate water measures :’ i. e.
 by the clepoydra, which was an instrument contrived to measure

 ﬂoated a piece of cork, which showed the hour. It was preferred
 by the ancients to the dial.

 διο. posterior. So the two centurions of the second manip e of

 and so on to the tenth, who was called centurio decimi pili, prior

 9. Municipia: foreign towns, which obtained the rights of ~
 Roman citizens, were called municipia-Colonias .- colonies were
 cities or lands, which Romans were sent to inhabit. They were
 commonly transplanted by three commissioners and sometimes
 by alm-ger number. The people determined in what manner,
 md to whom the lands were to be divided. The new colon
 marched to their destined place in form of an army, with co -
 ors ﬂying. The two chief mugitu-stes· of a. colony were called
 .Du’umvin', the senators, Decurwm, because, as some say, when
 the colony was ﬁrst planted, every tenth man was made a
 senator.
